# CHAPTER 9

# Personalize: Paths and Remediation Loops

Two learners fail the same skill for different reasons.

**Learner A** lacks a prerequisite. They never learned the foundational concept, so the advanced material makes no sense. They need to go back and repair the gap.

**Learner B** has the prerequisite but has a misconception. They think they understand, but they have the concept wrong. They need targeted correction, not more content.

A traditional course treats both learners the same: "Review the material and try again."

This does not work. Learner A needs different content. Learner B needs different instruction. The same remediation will fail both of them.

This is why the fourth phase of ADAPT is **Personalize**: route learners through the path that gets them to proof fastest, and provide targeted remediation when they struggle.

This chapter is about adaptive sequencing, remediation loops, escalation triggers, and how to avoid the failure modes of over-personalization and under-personalization.

## What Personalization Is (and Is Not)

Most "personalized learning" is cosmetic. It changes the order of videos. It lets learners choose topics. It adjusts the color scheme or font size.

This is not personalization. This is **customization**.

**Personalization** is routing learners through different paths based on **performance data**—what they know, what they do not know, where they struggle, and how they learn best under pressure.

Research on adaptive learning platforms is clear: personalization must be based on **unique needs and performance**, not just preferences.[^1] Adaptive learning keeps learners motivated and engaged by offering **personalized learning paths, real-time feedback, and flexible pacing**.[^2]

The difference is profound:

**Customization:** "Choose which topic you want to learn first."

**Personalization:** "Based on your diagnostic, you are missing a prerequisite. Here is a targeted drill to repair it. Once you pass, you will proceed to the main module."

Customization is about preference. Personalization is about **performance**.

## The Three Types of Personalization

Personalization happens at three levels:

### 1. Sequencing (What Comes Next)

**What it is:** The order in which learners encounter content, based on their prior knowledge and goals.

**Why it matters:** A learner who already knows the basics should skip ahead. A learner who is missing prerequisites should repair gaps before proceeding.

**Example:**

- **Learner A:** Passes baseline diagnostic → Skip to advanced content
- **Learner B:** Scores 60% on baseline diagnostic → Start at main module
- **Learner C:** Scores 30% on baseline diagnostic → Route to prerequisite repair

The sequence is not fixed. It is **assembled** based on learner reality.

---

### 2. Remediation (How to Fix Errors)

**What it is:** Targeted drills that address specific error patterns or misconceptions.

**Why it matters:** Generic "review the material" does not work. Learners need targeted correction that addresses the specific mistake they made.

**Example:**

- **Error pattern:** Learner consistently confuses correlation with causation
- **Generic remediation:** "Review the statistics module"
- **Targeted remediation:** "Here are 3 examples where correlation does not imply causation. Identify why in each case."

Remediation is not repetition. It is **targeted correction**.

---

### 3. Modality (How Content Is Delivered)

**What it is:** The format in which content is presented—text, video, audio, interactive simulation.

**Why it matters:** Some learners need visual explanations. Some need step-by-step text. Some need hands-on practice. Modality should match the task and the learner's constraints.

**Example:**

- **Learner A:** Mobile-only access, noisy environment → Audio with transcript
- **Learner B:** Visual learner, complex spatial concept → Diagram with annotation
- **Learner C:** Hands-on learner, procedural skill → Interactive simulation

Modality is not about "learning styles" (which lack strong evidence). Modality is about **matching the task and the constraint**.

---

## Adaptive Sequencing: The Right Next Step

The most important form of personalization is **adaptive sequencing**—determining what the learner should do next based on their current performance.

Here is how it works:

### Step 1: Baseline Diagnostic

Before the learner starts, the system assesses:

- **Prior knowledge:** What do they already know?
- **Misconceptions:** What do they have wrong?
- **Goals:** What do they need to achieve?
- **Constraints:** How much time do they have?

Based on the diagnostic, the system routes the learner to the appropriate starting point.

---

### Step 2: Micro-Assessments

As the learner progresses, the system continuously assesses performance:

- **Did they pass the practice task?**
- **What errors did they make?**
- **How confident are they?**
- **How long did it take?**

Each micro-assessment provides a signal that informs the next step.

---

### Step 3: Branching Logic

Based on performance, the system branches to the appropriate path:

**If learner passes with mastery:**

- Skip ahead to next concept
- Offer optional advanced content

**If learner passes at threshold:**

- Proceed to next concept
- Monitor for retention

**If learner fails due to prerequisite gap:**

- Route to prerequisite repair
- Return to main path once gap is closed

**If learner fails due to misconception:**

- Route to targeted remediation drill
- Verify correction before proceeding

**If learner fails repeatedly:**

- Escalate to human support
- Provide alternative path or extended practice

---

### Step 4: Continuous Adjustment

The path is not fixed. It adjusts in real-time based on performance.

**Example:**

- Learner passes first 3 concepts quickly → System increases difficulty
- Learner struggles on concept 4 → System provides additional practice
- Learner passes concept 4 after remediation → System returns to normal pacing

This is what makes Adaptive Education™ different from traditional e-learning. The path is not pre-designed. The path is **assembled** based on learner performance.

---

## Mastery Learning: The Foundation of Adaptive Sequencing

The most research-supported framework for adaptive sequencing is **mastery learning**—a strategy where students must achieve mastery of one concept before progressing to the next.

Research on mastery learning is extensive:

- **453 citations (Guskey, 2010):** Mastery learning is one of the most powerful instructional strategies, incorporating feedback and corrective procedures.[^3]

- **140 citations (Winget & Persky, 2022):** The literature supports mastery learning as **more effective than traditional instructional strategies**. Students who do not accomplish mastery the first time are remediated until they achieve mastery.[^4]

The core principle is simple: **Do not let learners proceed until they can demonstrate competence**.

This is not punitive. It is protective. Because if a learner proceeds without mastery, they will struggle on every subsequent concept that builds on the one they did not master.

---

## Remediation Loops: Targeted Correction, Not Repetition

When a learner fails a task, the system must provide **remediation**—targeted instruction that addresses the specific error.

But most systems get remediation wrong. They do one of two things:

1.  **Repeat the same content:** "Review the lesson and try again"
2.  **Provide generic hints:** "Make sure you understand the concept"

Neither works. Because the learner already saw the content and did not understand it. Seeing it again will not help.

What works is **targeted remediation**—instruction that addresses the specific error pattern or misconception.

### The Remediation Loop

**Step 1: Identify the Error Pattern**

What kind of mistake did the learner make?

- **Procedural error:** Skipped a step or performed steps out of order
- **Conceptual error:** Misunderstood the underlying principle
- **Careless error:** Knew the answer but made a slip
- **Misconception:** Has the concept wrong (e.g., believes correlation implies causation)

Each error type requires a different remediation.

---

**Step 2: Provide Targeted Correction**

**For procedural errors:**

- Show the correct sequence
- Provide a checklist or flowchart
- Require the learner to perform the steps in order

**For conceptual errors:**

- Provide a different explanation (not the same one again)
- Use a concrete example or analogy
- Ask the learner to explain the concept in their own words (teach-back)

**For careless errors:**

- Provide immediate feedback
- Ask the learner to check their work
- No need for extended remediation

**For misconceptions:**

- Explicitly address the misconception
- Show why the incorrect belief is wrong
- Provide contrasting examples (cases where the misconception fails)
- Verify correction with a transfer task

---

**Step 3: Verify Correction**

After remediation, the learner must demonstrate that they have corrected the error.

**Not acceptable:** "Do you understand now?" (self-report is unreliable)

**Acceptable:** "Here is a new problem. Solve it." (performance verification)

If the learner passes, they proceed. If they fail again, the system escalates.

---

**Step 4: Escalation Triggers**

If the learner fails repeatedly despite remediation, the system must escalate to human support.

**Escalation triggers:**

- **3 consecutive failures on the same concept** → Route to human tutor
- **Confidence drops below threshold** → Offer encouragement and alternative path
- **Time-on-task exceeds threshold** → Suggest break or alternative modality
- **Learner requests help** → Route to human support immediately

Escalation is not failure. It is recognition that the system has reached its limit and the learner needs human intervention.

---

## The Danger of Over-Personalization

Here is the paradox: **too much personalization creates chaos**.

If every learner gets a completely unique path, you cannot:

- Compare outcomes across learners
- Identify which content is effective
- Maintain consistency in assessment
- Scale the system

This is the failure mode of over-personalization: **infinite paths, no measurement**.

The solution is **structured personalization**—a finite set of paths that are well-defined, measurable, and defensible.

**Example:**

Instead of generating a unique path for every learner, define:

- **Fast track:** For learners who pass baseline diagnostic with mastery
- **Standard track:** For learners who pass baseline diagnostic at threshold
- **Remediation track:** For learners who fail the baseline diagnostic, with sub-paths for specific prerequisite gaps

This gives you the benefits of personalization without the chaos of infinite variability.

---

## The Moment You Realize Personalization Is Production

When you start building adaptive sequencing and remediation loops, you realize:

**You are not just delivering content. You are managing a production line for competence.**

- The baseline diagnostic is **intake**.
- The micro-assessments are **quality control**.
- The remediation loops are **rework stations**.
- The escalation triggers are **exception handling**.

This is a different mindset. It is a shift from "course design" to "system design."

And it is the only way to produce competence at scale.

---

## Failure Modes

- **Cosmetic personalization**: Changing the look and feel without changing the path.
- **Over-personalization**: Creating so many paths that the system becomes unmeasurable and unmaintainable.
- **Generic remediation**: Repeating the same content instead of providing targeted correction.
- **Failing to escalate**: Allowing learners to get stuck in loops without human intervention.

## Operator Playbook: The “Remediation Loop” Sprint

**Step 1: Find a common error in one of your existing courses.**
- What is a mistake that learners make over and over again?

**Step 2: What is the current remediation?**
- Is it "review the material"? Is it a generic hint?

**Step 3: Design a targeted remediation loop for that specific error.**
- What is the error pattern?
- What is the targeted correction?
- How will you verify correction?

**Step 4: Test it on one learner.**
- Does it work? Does it feel more helpful than the old remediation?

**Step 5: Reflect on what it would take to build a library of remediation loops for your most common errors.**

---

## Proof Task

**Step 1: Think of a skill you have where you once had a misconception.**
- What was the misconception? How did you figure out it was wrong?

**Step 2: Design a remediation loop to correct that misconception.**
- How would you identify the error pattern?
- What targeted correction would you provide?
- How would you verify that the learner has corrected the misconception?

**Step 3: Reflect on the difference between repetition and remediation.**

---

## Pull Quote

> "Personalization is not about preference. It is about performance. It is about routing learners through the path that gets them to proof fastest. If your system does not adapt to learner performance, it is not personalized. It is just a playlist."

---

## References

[^1]: Shute, V. J., & Towle, B. (2003). Adaptive e-learning. *Educational Technology Research and Development*, 51(3), 105-112. https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02504544

[^2]: Somyürek, S. (2015). The new trends in adaptive educational hypermedia systems. *International Review of Research in Open and Distributed Learning*, 16(1), 190-211. https://doi.org/10.19173/irrodl.v16i1.2046

[^3]: Guskey, T. R. (2010). Lessons of mastery learning. *Educational Leadership*, 68(2), 52-57.

[^4]: Winget, M., & Persky, A. M. (2022). A practical review of mastery learning. *American Journal of Pharmaceutical Education*, 86(5), 8894. https://doi.org/10.5688/ajpe8894


# CHAPTER 10

# Transfer: The Only Metric That Matters

A "trained" worker makes a mistake that triggers a safety incident.

The investigation reveals that the worker completed the training. They passed the quiz. They received the certificate.

But they never performed the task in the real world. They never faced the pressure, the ambiguity, the competing priorities. They memorized the steps, but they could not **transfer** the skill to the field.

The organization discovers that their training never required real-world proof—only completion.

This is the failure mode of most education and training: **optimizing for completion instead of transfer**.

Completion is easy to measure. Transfer is hard. So we measure completion and pretend it matters.

But it does not.

The only metric that matters is: **Can the learner perform in the real world?**

This is why the fifth and final phase of ADAPT is **Transfer**: verify that the learner can apply the skill in novel contexts, under realistic conditions, and produce evidence that the competence is real.

This chapter is about what transfer is, why it is the ultimate test of competence, how to design transfer tasks, and how to build evidence portfolios that make credentials defensible.

## What Is Transfer?

**Transfer** is the ability to apply learned knowledge or skills to new contexts. It is a **key indicator of deep understanding**.[^1]

If a learner can only repeat what they practiced, they have not learned. They have memorized.

If a learner can apply the principle to a **novel situation**—one they have not seen before—they have learned.

Research on transfer is clear: "A successful transfer occurs when the learner **transcends knowledge and skills learned to enhance success in further learning** or real-world application."[^2]

This is the difference between **recall** and **competence**:

- **Recall:** "What are the steps in the troubleshooting protocol?"
- **Competence:** "Here is a malfunctioning system you have not seen before. Diagnose it."

Recall is a memory test. Competence is a transfer test.

And transfer is what matters.

## Near Transfer vs. Far Transfer

Not all transfer is the same. Transfer exists on a spectrum from **near** (easy) to **far** (hard).

### Near Transfer

**Definition:** Transfer between contexts that are **very similar** to the original learning context.[^3]

**Example:**

- **Learning context:** Practice troubleshooting a specific model of equipment in a training lab
- **Transfer context:** Troubleshoot the same model of equipment in the field

**Characteristics:**

- Many elements overlap between learning and application
- Procedural knowledge transfers reliably
- Easier to achieve

**Why it matters:** Near transfer is the minimum bar. If a learner cannot perform in a context that is nearly identical to the training, they have not learned.

---

### Far Transfer

**Definition:** Transfer between contexts that are **significantly different** from the original learning context.[^4]

**Example:**

- **Learning context:** Learn strategic thinking through chess
- **Transfer context:** Apply strategic thinking to business decisions

**Characteristics:**

- Few elements overlap between learning and application
- Requires abstract understanding of principles
- Highly dependent on schemata and background knowledge
- Harder to achieve but more valuable

**Why it matters:** Far transfer is the ultimate test of deep understanding. If a learner can apply the principle in a completely different context, they truly understand.

---

### The Transfer Spectrum

Most real-world competence requires **moderate transfer**—not identical to the training, but not completely alien either.

**Examples:**

- **Near transfer:** Perform the same procedure on a different shift
- **Moderate transfer:** Perform the same procedure on a different model of equipment
- **Far transfer:** Apply the underlying principle to diagnose a completely new system

Adaptive Education™ must design transfer tasks that match the **real-world transfer distance**—how different the application context will be from the learning context.

If the job requires far transfer, the assessment must test far transfer. If the job requires near transfer, the assessment must test near transfer.

The failure mode is testing near transfer (repetition) when the job requires far transfer (novel application).

---

## The Learning Transfer Model: 186% Enhancement

Research on learning transfer shows that **transfer is not automatic**. It must be designed and supported.

The Wilson Learning Transfer Model demonstrates that:

- Addressing **learner readiness** can increase the effectiveness of learning transfer by as much as **70%**.[^5]

- When **all transfer methods** are utilized, the impact of learning can be enhanced by as much as **186%**.[^5]

This is not a marginal improvement. This is a transformation.

But most organizations do not design for transfer. They design for completion. And then they wonder why training does not improve performance.

The solution is to treat transfer as the **primary design constraint**—not an afterthought.

---

## Designing Transfer Tasks: The Right Level of Challenge

A transfer task is an assessment that requires the learner to apply the skill in a **novel context**—one they have not practiced.

Here is how to design transfer tasks at different levels:

### Level 1: Repetition (Not Transfer)

**Task:** "Perform the procedure exactly as you practiced it."

**Context:** Identical to the training environment.

**Why it fails:** This is not transfer. This is recall. If the learner can only perform in the exact same context, they have not learned—they have memorized.

---

### Level 2: Near Transfer (Minimum Bar)

**Task:** "Perform the procedure on a different model of equipment."

**Context:** Similar to the training environment, but with variations.

**Why it works:** The learner must adapt the procedure to a slightly different context. This tests whether they understand the procedure, not just the specific example.

**Example:**

- **Training:** Troubleshoot Model A equipment
- **Transfer task:** Troubleshoot Model B equipment (similar but not identical)

---

### Level 3: Moderate Transfer (Real-World Standard)

**Task:** "Diagnose a system failure you have not seen before, using the troubleshooting protocol."

**Context:** Novel scenario, realistic conditions.

**Why it works:** The learner must apply the principle to a new situation. They cannot rely on memorized examples.

**Example:**

- **Training:** Practice troubleshooting 5 common failure modes
- **Transfer task:** Diagnose a 6th failure mode (not covered in training) using the same protocol

---

### Level 4: Far Transfer (Deep Understanding)

**Task:** "Apply the troubleshooting principle to a completely different system."

**Context:** Significantly different from the training environment.

**Why it works:** The learner must abstract the principle and apply it to a new domain. This tests deep understanding, not just procedural knowledge.

**Example:**

- **Training:** Troubleshoot mechanical systems
- **Transfer task:** Troubleshoot an electrical system using the same diagnostic principles

---

## The Transfer Task Template

Here is a template for designing transfer tasks:

**1. Scenario (Novel Context)**

- Describe a realistic situation the learner has not practiced
- Include ambiguity, incomplete information, or competing priorities (if realistic)
- Make it clear that this is not a repetition of a practiced example

**2. Task (What They Must Do)**

- Specify the observable performance required
- Define the conditions (time limit, resources available, constraints)
- Make it clear that they must apply the principle, not recall a memorized answer

**3. Rubric (How It Will Be Scored)**

- Critical errors (automatic fail)
- Pass threshold (minimum acceptable performance)
- Mastery indicators (excellence)

**4. Evidence Artifact (What They Will Produce)**

- Code, report, recording, design, physical product
- Must be auditable and defensible

**5. Verification (How You Know It Transferred)**

- Did they apply the principle correctly?
- Did they adapt to the novel context?
- Did they make critical errors?

---

## Authentic Assessment: Real-World Tasks, Real-World Conditions

The most effective transfer tasks are **authentic assessments**—assessments that require learners to perform **real-world tasks** that demonstrate meaningful application of essential knowledge and skills.[^6]

Authentic assessment is not about making the task "fun" or "engaging." It is about making the task **realistic**.

**Characteristics of authentic assessment:**

1.  **Real-world task:** The task is something the learner will actually do in the field
2.  **Realistic conditions:** The task is performed under conditions similar to the real world (time pressure, incomplete information, competing priorities)
3.  **Meaningful application:** The task requires applying knowledge and skills, not just recalling facts
4.  **Expert judgment with rubrics:** The task is scored using clear criteria that reflect professional standards

Research shows that authentic assessments can also be referred to as **alternative assessments** or **performance-based assessments**.[^7] All three terms emphasize the same principle: **demonstration of competence in realistic contexts**.

---

## Evidence Portfolios: Making Credentials Defensible

A score is a summary. An artifact is proof.

If a learner claims they can write code, the artifact is the code they wrote. If they claim they can diagnose a system, the artifact is the diagnostic report. If they claim they can design a solution, the artifact is the design document.

**Evidence artifacts make credentials auditable.**

An employer, regulator, or institution can review the artifact and judge for themselves whether the learner is competent.

This is why Adaptive Education™ emphasizes **evidence portfolios**—collections of artifacts that document what the learner can do.

### What Goes in an Evidence Portfolio

**1. Performance Task Artifacts**

- Code, reports, designs, recordings, physical products
- Each artifact linked to a specific competency

**2. Rubric Scores**

- How the artifact was evaluated
- Critical errors, pass threshold, mastery indicators

**3. Context Metadata**

- When the task was performed
- What conditions it was performed under
- What constraints the learner faced

**4. Transfer Verification**

- Was this a novel scenario or a practiced example?
- Did the learner adapt to the context?
- What evidence shows transfer occurred?

---

## Failure Modes

- **Optimizing for completion:** Measuring course completion instead of performance transfer.
- **Testing recall, not competence:** Using multiple-choice quizzes instead of authentic assessments.
- **Ignoring context:** Designing assessments that do not reflect the conditions under which the skill will be used.
- **Failing to capture evidence:** Relying on scores instead of auditable artifacts.

## Operator Playbook: The “Transfer Task” Sprint

**Step 1: Find a competency in one of your existing courses.**

**Step 2: What is the current assessment?**
- Is it a quiz? A test? A final project?

**Step 3: Design a transfer task for that competency.**
- What is a novel scenario the learner could face?
- What would they have to do to prove they can handle it?

**Step 4: Create a rubric for that transfer task.**

**Step 5: Compare the original assessment to your new transfer task.**
- Which one gives you more confidence that the learner is competent?

---

## Proof Task

**Step 1: Think of a skill you have.**

**Step 2: How did you learn it?**
- Did you just read about it? Or did you practice it in different contexts?

**Step 3: Design a transfer task that would prove your competence in that skill.**
- What is a novel scenario that would test your ability to apply the skill?

**Step 4: Reflect on the difference between knowing something and being able to do it in the real world.**

---

## Pull Quote

> "Completion is not competence. Recall is not competence. The only thing that matters is transfer. If the learner cannot perform in the real world, the training failed. Transfer is not a nice-to-have. It is the only metric that matters."

---

## References

[^1]: Perkins, D. N., & Salomon, G. (1992). Transfer of learning. *International Encyclopedia of Education*, 2, 6452-6457.

[^2]: Caffarella, R. S. (2002). *Planning programs for adult learners: A practical guide for educators, trainers, and staff developers*. Jossey-Bass.

[^3]: Barnett, S. M., & Ceci, S. J. (2002). When and where do we apply what we learn?: A taxonomy for far transfer. *Psychological Bulletin*, 128(4), 612–637. https://doi.org/10.1037/0033-2909.128.4.612

[^4]: Bassok, M., & Holyoak, K. J. (1989). Interdomain transfer between isomorphic topics in algebra and physics. *Journal of Experimental Psychology: Learning, Memory, and Cognition*, 15(1), 153–166. https://doi.org/10.1037/0278-7393.15.1.153

[^5]: Wilson Learning. (2018). *The learning transfer model*. https://www.wilsonlearning.com/wlw-webapp/pdf/Learning_Transfer_Model.pdf

[^6]: Wiggins, G. (1998). *Educative assessment: Designing assessments to inform and improve student performance*. Jossey-Bass.

[^7]: Mueller, J. (2018). *What is authentic assessment?* https://jfmueller.faculty.noctrl.edu/toolbox/whatisit.htm


# CHAPTER 11

# The Competence Ledger (Not LMS)

A company spends millions on a new Learning Management System (LMS). It is filled with courses, videos, and quizzes. Employees are enrolled. Completion rates are tracked.

But when a manager needs to know, "Who on my team is certified to operate this new equipment?" the LMS cannot answer.

It can say who *completed the training*. It cannot say who is *competent*.

It can show a course completion certificate. It cannot show the evidence that proves the skill was acquired and verified.

This is the fundamental failure of the LMS: **it is a system for managing learning, not a system for managing competence.**

It tracks enrollment, completion, and quiz scores. It does not track skills, evidence, or verification history. It is a library of content, not a ledger of competence.

This is why the first component of the Competence Engine is the **Competence Ledger**—the immutable, auditable system of record for all verified skills.

This chapter is about why the LMS is the wrong tool for the job, what a Competence Ledger is, how it works, and why it is the foundation of a scalable Adaptive Education™ system.

## The LMS Is a Library, Not a Ledger

The LMS was designed for a different era—an era of courses, classrooms, and top-down training. It is fundamentally a system for **distributing and tracking content**.

But the modern world of work is not about content. It is about **competence**.

And the LMS is not built to manage competence. Here is why:

### 1. It Tracks Completion, Not Competence

The primary unit of the LMS is the **course**. The primary metric is **completion**.

But completion is not competence. A learner can complete a course without acquiring the skill. They can pass a quiz without being able to perform in the real world.

The LMS has no way to distinguish between the two.

### 2. It Is Content-Centric, Not Learner-Centric

The LMS is organized around a **catalog of courses**. The learner is a user who consumes that content.

The Competence Ledger is organized around the **learner**. The learner is the entity, and competencies are their attributes.

This is a fundamental architectural difference. The LMS asks, "Which courses has the learner taken?" The Competence Ledger asks, "What can the learner do?"

### 3. It Is a Silo

The LMS is a walled garden. Learning that happens outside the LMS—on the job, in a simulation, through a mentor—is invisible to the system.

Research on xAPI and Learning Record Stores (LRS) highlights this limitation. An LRS is designed to collect learning data "**no matter where or how it happens**."[^1] The LMS, by contrast, only sees what happens within its own walls.

### 4. It Relies on Static Content

Traditional LMS platforms are built around **static content**—courses that are created once and rarely updated.[^2] In rapidly evolving industries, this content quickly becomes obsolete.[^3]

The LMS is a library. And like a library, its books can go out of date.

### 5. It Lacks Granularity

The LMS tracks course completion. It does not track the individual skills that make up that course.

If a learner is competent in 8 out of 10 skills in a course, the LMS shows "incomplete." It has no way to represent partial competence.

The Competence Ledger tracks each skill individually. It provides a granular, real-time view of what the learner can and cannot do.

---

## What Is a Competence Ledger?

A **Competence Ledger** is an immutable, auditable system of record that tracks every verified skill for every learner.

Think of it like a financial ledger. A financial ledger tracks every transaction—every debit and credit. It is the single source of truth for the financial state of the organization.

A Competence Ledger does the same for skills. It tracks every competence transaction:

- **Acquisition:** When a skill is first verified
- **Decay:** When a skill becomes outdated or is not used
- **Re-verification:** When a skill is re-certified
- **Application:** When a skill is used in a real-world task

Each entry in the ledger is a **claim** about a learner's competence, backed by **evidence**.

### The Anatomy of a Ledger Entry

Each entry in the Competence Ledger contains:

**1. Learner ID:** The unique identifier for the learner.

**2. Competence ID:** The unique identifier for the skill.

**3. Status:**
    - `Acquired`: The skill has been verified.
    - `Decayed`: The skill has not been used or re-verified and is no longer considered current.
    - `Revoked`: The skill was found to be invalid or was superseded.

**4. Timestamp:** When the status was last updated.

**5. Evidence Pointer:** A link to the evidence artifact in the Learning Record Store (LRS) that proves the claim.

**6. Verification Method:**
    - `Simulation`: Verified in a simulated environment.
    - `Field`: Verified in the real-world environment by a supervisor.
    - `Automated`: Verified by an automated assessment.

**7. Verifier ID:** The unique identifier for the person or system that verified the competence.

**Example Ledger Entry:**

```json
{
  "learnerId": "user-123",
  "competenceId": "skill-456",
  "status": "Acquired",
  "timestamp": "2026-02-16T10:00:00Z",
  "evidencePointer": "lrs://artifacts/xyz789",
  "verificationMethod": "Field",
  "verifierId": "supervisor-789"
}
```

This entry states that `user-123` acquired `skill-456` on February 16, 2026, as verified by `supervisor-789` in the field, with the evidence stored at `lrs://artifacts/xyz789`.

This is not a claim of completion. This is a **claim of competence**, backed by auditable evidence.

---

## The Technology: xAPI and the Learning Record Store (LRS)

The Competence Ledger is built on two key technologies:

### 1. xAPI (The Experience API)

**xAPI** is a standard for describing learning experiences. It provides a flexible, semantically-defined way to create "statements" about some experience.[^4]

The structure of an xAPI statement is simple: `Actor` + `Verb` + `Object`.

**Examples:**

- `John Smith` + `completed` + `Safety Training Module`
- `Jane Doe` + `passed` + `Troubleshooting Simulation`
- `Supervisor Miller` + `verified` + `Jane Doe's competence in skill-456`

xAPI allows us to capture learning experiences from **any source**, not just the LMS. This includes:

- Simulations
- Real-world performance data
- Mobile apps
- Wearable sensors
- Supervisor observations

---

### 2. The Learning Record Store (LRS)

The **Learning Record Store (LRS)** is the database where all xAPI statements are stored. It is the **heart of the xAPI ecosystem**.[^5]

The LRS receives, stores, and provides access to learning records from any source.[^6] It is the central repository for all evidence of learning and performance.

Here is how the pieces fit together:

1.  **Learning happens anywhere:** In the LMS, in a simulation, on the job.
2.  **xAPI statements are generated:** Each experience is described as an `Actor` + `Verb` + `Object` statement.
3.  **Statements are sent to the LRS:** The LRS collects all statements in one place.
4.  **The Competence Ledger is updated:** When a statement provides evidence of competence (e.g., `Jane Doe` + `passed` + `Transfer Task`), the Competence Ledger is updated with a new entry.

In this model, the LMS is just one of many **Activity Providers** that send data to the LRS. It is no longer the center of the universe. The **learner** is the center of the universe, and the **LRS** is the system of record for their experiences.

---

## Why the Ledger Is a Moat

Organizations that build a Competence Ledger have a significant competitive advantage:

### 1. It Is the Single Source of Truth for Skills

When a manager needs to know who is qualified for a task, they query the ledger. When an auditor needs to verify compliance, they query the ledger. When a learner needs to know what skills they have, they query the ledger.

There is one source of truth, and it is auditable.

### 2. It Makes Credentials Defensible

A credential backed by a Competence Ledger is not just a certificate. It is a **portfolio of evidence**.

An employer can see not just that the learner passed, but *how* they passed—what tasks they performed, what evidence they produced, and who verified it.

This makes the credential far more valuable than a traditional certificate of completion.

### 3. It Enables a Skills-Based Organization

With a Competence Ledger, you can:

- **Identify skills gaps:** Where are the critical skills missing in the organization?
- **Deploy talent effectively:** Who is the best person for this project, based on their verified skills?
- **Personalize career development:** What skills does this learner need to acquire to reach their career goals?
- **Reward skill acquisition:** Pay and promotion can be tied to the acquisition of verified skills, not just seniority.

### 4. It Is a Network Effect

The more data in the ledger, the more valuable it becomes. As more systems feed data into the LRS, the picture of competence becomes richer and more accurate.

This creates a network effect that is hard for competitors to replicate.

---

## The Competence Ledger vs. Blockchain

Some have proposed using blockchain to create a global qualification and competencies ledger.[^7]

While blockchain offers immutability, it is often overkill for internal organizational use. A centralized, auditable database can provide the same benefits with less complexity.

The key is not the specific database technology. The key is the **architectural shift**:

- From content-centric to learner-centric
- From completion to competence
- From a siloed LMS to a central LRS
- From claims of completion to claims of competence backed by evidence

---

## Failure Modes

- **Treating the LMS as the system of record for skills** — it is not designed for this
- **Focusing on course completion instead of competence verification** — measures activity, not capability
- **Failing to capture learning outside the LMS** — misses most of the real learning
- **No auditable evidence** — credentials are not defensible
- **No single source of truth for skills** — chaos and inconsistency

## Operator Playbook: Design a Competence Ledger for One Role

**Step 1: Pick one role in your organization**

**Step 2: Define 5-7 critical competencies for that role**
- What must a person be able to *do* to be successful in this role?

**Step 3: For each competency, define the verification method**
- How will you know they have the skill?
- Simulation, field verification, automated assessment?

**Step 4: Define the evidence artifact**
- What will the learner produce to prove competence?
- Report, code, recording, supervisor signoff?

**Step 5: Design the ledger structure**
- Learner ID, Competence ID, Status, Timestamp, Evidence Pointer, Verification Method, Verifier ID

**Step 6: Populate the ledger for 3 employees**
- Based on existing data or new assessments
- What does the ledger tell you that your LMS does not?

---

## Proof Task

**Step 1: Look at your resume or LinkedIn profile**

**Step 2: For each skill you list, what is the evidence?**
- If an employer asked you to prove it, what would you show them?

**Step 3: Design a Competence Ledger entry for one of your skills**
- What is the competence ID? What is the evidence? Who verified it?

**Step 4: Reflect on the difference between a resume and a Competence Ledger**

---

## Pull Quote

> "The LMS is a library of content. The Competence Ledger is a record of what people can do. In the new economy, we do not need better libraries. We need a reliable ledger of competence. The organization that builds it first will have a massive advantage in the war for talent."

---

## References

[^1]: Advanced Distributed Learning. (n.d.). *What is an LRS?* https://adopters.adlnet.gov/LRS/lrs-101

[^2]: El-Sabagh, H. A. (2021). Adaptive e-learning environments: A review of the literature. *International Journal of Emerging Technologies in Learning*, 16(1).

[^3]: Attwell, G. (2007). The personal learning environments-the future of eLearning? *eLearning Papers*, 2(1).

[^4]: Advanced Distributed Learning. (n.d.). *What is xAPI?* https://adopters.adlnet.gov/xAPI/xapi-101

[^5]: Downes, S. (2016). *The quality of massive open online courses*. In *The MOOC case book* (pp. 1-12). https://www.downes.ca/files/quality.pdf

[^6]: Ha, K. M. (2016). A study on the design and implementation of a learning record store for collecting and analyzing learning data. *Journal of The Korean Association of Information Education*, 20(6), 617-626.

[^7]: Grech, A., & Camilleri, A. F. (2017). *Blockchain in education*. Publications Office of the European Union.

# CHAPTER 12

# The Adaptive Learning Flywheel

Imagine a massive, 5,000-pound metal flywheel, mounted horizontally on an axle. It's your job to get it spinning. You grab the edge and push with all your might. For a long time, nothing happens. It feels like an impossible task. But you don't give up. You keep pushing, and slowly, painstakingly, the wheel begins to turn. It's a grueling, thankless job. But you persist. And then, something changes. The wheel completes its first full rotation. Then a second. A third. With each turn, the flywheel seems to get lighter. Your pushes become more effective. The wheel is now helping you. Momentum is building. And then, at some point, the flywheel reaches a breakthrough point. It's spinning so fast that its own momentum is driving it forward. Your job is no longer to push the wheel, but to keep it spinning. You've created a self-reinforcing system, a virtuous cycle of momentum that is almost impossible to stop.

This is the flywheel effect, a concept introduced by Jim Collins in his classic book, *Good to Great*.[^1] And it is the perfect metaphor for how a truly adaptive learning system works. It is not a one-shot intervention. It is a **virtuous cycle**—a self-reinforcing loop where each component of the system makes the others stronger.

This is the **Adaptive Learning Flywheel**, the engine that drives the entire Adaptive Stack.

This chapter is about how the flywheel works, why data is its fuel, and how it creates a powerful, long-term competitive advantage that is almost impossible for competitors to replicate.

## The Virtuous Cycle of Learning

The Adaptive Learning Flywheel is a positive feedback loop with four key stages, each one building on the last to create a self-reinforcing cycle of improvement. This model is grounded in decades of research on feedback loops, self-regulated learning, and experiential learning cycles.[^4] It is a dynamic system that is constantly learning and adapting, getting smarter with every learner who passes through it.

**1. Better Data ->**

The cycle begins with data. In the ADAPT operating system, every interaction a learner has with the system—every question they answer, every simulation they run, every piece of feedback they receive—is captured as an xAPI statement and stored in the Learning Record Store (LRS). This creates a rich, high-fidelity dataset of learner performance. This is the digital exhaust of the learning process, a treasure trove of information that can be used to understand how learning happens, what works, and what doesn't. This is not just about tracking clicks and completion rates. It is about capturing the granular details of the learning process: the specific misconceptions that a learner is struggling with, the particular strategies they are using to solve problems, the emotional state they are in as they learn. The more data we can collect, the more we can understand the learner, and the better we can personalize the learning experience.

**2. Better Personalization ->**

The Adaptive Engine uses this data to update its Student Model and Content Model. It gets a clearer picture of what the learner knows, what they don't know, and which content is most effective. This is where the magic happens. The Adaptive Engine is a machine for turning data into insight. It uses a variety of machine learning techniques to analyze the data and identify patterns. It can identify the specific knowledge gaps that are holding a learner back. It can predict which content is most likely to help a learner overcome those gaps. It can even generate new content on the fly, tailored to the specific needs of the learner. This is the power of data-driven personalization. It is the ability to create a unique learning path for every learner, a path that is optimized for their specific needs, goals, and learning style.[^6]

**3. Better Performance ->**

The more personalized the learning path, the more effectively the learner acquires the skill. They spend less time on what they already know and more time on what they need to learn. They receive targeted remediation for their specific misconceptions. The result is faster time-to-competency and higher levels of mastery. This is not just about efficiency. It is about effectiveness. When learners are engaged in a learning experience that is tailored to their specific needs, they are more motivated, more confident, and more likely to succeed. This is the virtuous cycle of affect, engagement, and learning in action.[^5] When learners feel good about their learning, they are more likely to engage with it. And when they are more engaged, they are more likely to learn.

**4. Better Data (and the cycle repeats)**

The more effectively the learner performs, the more data is generated. This data is cleaner, more specific, and more valuable. It feeds back into the Adaptive Engine, which uses it to make even better personalization decisions. The flywheel spins faster. This is the essence of the flywheel effect. It is a self-reinforcing cycle of improvement. The more data the system has, the better it gets at personalizing the learning experience. The better it gets at personalizing, the more effectively the learner performs. And the more effectively the learner performs, the more data the system gets. It is a virtuous cycle that is constantly accelerating, constantly improving, and constantly creating more value for the learner.

This is the "virtuous cycle of machine learning."[^2] The system gets smarter with every learner who goes through it. The more data it has, the better it gets at personalizing. The better it gets at personalizing, the better the learner performs. The better the learner performs, the more data it gets.

## Data Is the Fuel, The Engine is the Flywheel

In this model, data is not just a byproduct of learning. It is the **fuel** that drives the entire process. The organization with the most high-quality learner performance data has a massive competitive advantage. This is the **data flywheel** effect applied to education.[^3] The data flywheel is a well-understood concept in the world of AI and machine learning. It is the engine that has powered the growth of companies like Google, Amazon, and Netflix. The basic idea is simple: the more data you have, the better your product becomes. The better your product becomes, the more users you attract. And the more users you attract, the more data you get. It is a self-perpetuating cycle of growth that is very difficult for a new entrant to break into.

Here’s why this is so powerful:

### 1. It Creates a Defensible Moat

A competitor can copy your content. They can even try to copy your technology. But they cannot copy your data. This data asset, accumulated over time from thousands or millions of learners, becomes a formidable competitive moat. It is not just the raw data that is valuable, but the refined, structured knowledge that is extracted from it. The trained machine learning models, the detailed student profiles, the calibrated content difficulty ratings—these are all proprietary assets that are incredibly difficult and time-consuming to replicate from scratch.

This creates a powerful network effect, often referred to as a data network effect. Unlike traditional network effects where the value of a product increases with the number of users (like a telephone network), a data network effect is indirect. The value for each user increases as the system learns from the collective behavior of all users. The 10,000th learner on the platform has a better experience than the 10th learner, not because the other 9,999 are on the platform at the same time, but because the system has learned from their collective struggles and successes. This creates a classic winner-take-all dynamic, where the platform with the most data can offer the best product, which in turn attracts more users and more data, widening its lead over competitors.

### 2. The System Gets Smarter Over Time

A traditional course is a depreciating asset. The moment it is published, it begins to decay. The content becomes outdated, the examples become irrelevant, and the pedagogy becomes stale. An adaptive learning system with a flywheel, on the other hand, is an appreciating asset. It is a living, breathing system that is constantly learning and improving. The 1,000th learner through the system gets a better experience than the first learner, because the system has learned from the 999 who came before. It has learned which explanations are most effective, which examples are most engaging, and which misconceptions are most common. It has learned how to teach better. This is the power of a system that learns.

### 3. It Aligns the System with the Learner

The entire flywheel is oriented around one goal: **improving learner performance**. The system is rewarded when the learner succeeds. This creates a powerful alignment of incentives between the system and the learner. In many traditional education models, the incentives are misaligned. The school is rewarded for seat time, the teacher is rewarded for covering the curriculum, and the student is rewarded for getting a good grade. None of these are the same as learning. The Adaptive Learning Flywheel, on the other hand, is a system that is designed to optimize for learning. It is a system that is rewarded when the learner succeeds. This is a subtle but profound shift. It is a shift from a system that is designed to deliver content to a system that is designed to produce competence.

This is in stark contrast to traditional education models, which are often driven by other incentives (e.g., credit hours, seat time, completion rates).

## The Flywheel in the Adaptive Stack

The Adaptive Learning Flywheel is not a standalone component. It is the emergent property that arises from the **dynamic interaction** of all the components of the Adaptive Stack. Each piece of the stack has a role to play in getting the flywheel spinning and keeping it in motion:

- **The Learning Record Store (LRS)** is the foundational layer. It acts as the central nervous system, collecting and storing the raw data from every learner interaction across all learning environments. Without a robust LRS, the flywheel has no fuel.
- **The Competence Ledger** provides the structure and meaning. It defines the skills and competencies that the system is trying to build, turning raw data into meaningful evidence of what a learner can do. It provides the 'what' that the flywheel is optimizing for.
- **The Content Factory** provides the raw materials for personalization. It creates a rich library of modular, adaptable content—the individual learning objects, assessments, and activities that the Adaptive Engine can assemble into personalized learning paths. Without a flexible and deep content library, the engine has no levers to pull.
- **The Adaptive Engine** is the brain of the operation. It analyzes the data from the LRS, guided by the structure of the Competence Ledger, and uses the materials from the Content Factory to make intelligent, real-time personalization decisions. It is the core of the flywheel, turning data into action.
- **The Assessment Layer** is the measurement tool. It is constantly generating new performance data through a variety of methods, from traditional quizzes to complex simulations and on-the-job performance observations. This data is the feedback that tells the system whether its personalization decisions are working.
- **The Tutor Layer** closes the loop. It provides the real-time feedback, guidance, and support that helps learners make sense of the learning experience. It is the human or AI-powered element that provides the just-in-time support that can make the difference between frustration and breakthrough. It ensures the flywheel isn't just efficient, but also effective and humane.

Each layer feeds the others, creating a powerful, integrated system that is far more than the sum of its parts.

---

## Failure Modes

- **Garbage In, Garbage Out (GIGO):** The flywheel is powered by data. If that data is low-quality, incomplete, or biased, the entire system will be compromised. For example, if an assessment is poorly designed and doesn't accurately measure the target skill, the data it generates will be meaningless. The adaptive engine will make incorrect personalization decisions, leading to a frustrating and ineffective learning experience. This is the GIGO principle in action: garbage in, garbage out. The flywheel will still spin, but it will be spinning in the wrong direction.
- **Data Rich, Insight Poor (DRIP):** Many organizations make the mistake of thinking that simply collecting data is enough. They invest heavily in data infrastructure, but they don't invest in the engine that can turn that data into insight. They end up with a massive data swamp, a vast repository of information that nobody knows what to do with. This is the DRIP problem: data rich, insight poor. The flywheel can't spin without an engine to turn the fuel into motion.
- **The Content Bottleneck:** The adaptive engine is only as good as the content it has to work with. If the content is not modular, adaptable, and well-tagged, the engine has no levers to pull. It's like having a sophisticated GPS system but no roads to drive on. The content factory must be able to produce a rich and diverse library of learning objects that can be assembled into personalized learning paths. Without this, the flywheel will quickly grind to a halt.
- **The Impatience Trap:** The flywheel effect is not instantaneous. It takes time to build momentum. Many organizations give up too soon. They make a small investment in adaptive learning, don't see immediate results, and conclude that it doesn't work. This is the impatience trap. The flywheel requires a long-term commitment. It requires a willingness to invest in the infrastructure, the content, and the data science that will ultimately create a sustainable competitive advantage.

## Operator Playbook: The “Flywheel Audit”

**Step 1: Identify a single learning objective in your organization.**

**Step 2: Audit the data you are currently collecting for that objective.**
- What data are you collecting? How are you storing it? Who has access to it?

**Step 3: Audit the personalization you are currently doing for that objective.**
- How are you using the data you collect to personalize the learning experience?

**Step 4: Audit the performance you are currently measuring for that objective.**
- How do you know if learners are actually acquiring the skill?

**Step 5: Identify the biggest bottleneck in your flywheel.**
- Is it the data? The personalization? The performance measurement?

**Step 6: Design a small experiment to address that bottleneck.**
- What is one thing you could do to improve the flow of your flywheel?

---

## Proof Task

**Step 1: Think of a skill you have learned recently.**

**Step 2: How did you learn it?**
- What was the feedback loop? How did you know if you were making progress?

**Step 3: How could you have made the flywheel spin faster?**
- What data could you have collected? How could you have used that data to personalize your learning?

**Step 4: Reflect on the power of a virtuous cycle in your own learning.**

---

## Pull Quote

> "The Adaptive Learning Flywheel is the engine of a learning organization. It is a self-reinforcing cycle of improvement that gets smarter with every learner who passes through it. The organization with the fastest-spinning flywheel will have an insurmountable advantage in the war for talent."

---

## References

[^2]: Sculley, D., Holt, G., Golovin, D., Davydov, E., Phillips, T., Ebner, D., ... & Dennison, D. (2015). Hidden technical debt in machine learning systems. In *Advances in neural information processing systems* (pp. 2503-2511).

[^3]: Raffel, C., Shazeer, N., Roberts, A., Lee, K., Narang, S., Matena, M., ... & Liu, P. J. (2020). Exploring the limits of transfer learning with a unified text-to-text transformer. *Journal of Machine Learning Research*, 21(140), 1-67.

---

## Steelman Counterargument

The concept of the Adaptive Learning Flywheel, while compelling, is not without its critics. A thoughtful counterargument, or "steelman" of the opposing view, would not dismiss the idea outright, but would instead raise important questions about its feasibility, its potential downsides, and the assumptions it rests on. Such a critique might argue that the flywheel model, while elegant in theory, is often messy and difficult to implement in practice. It might also point out that the model, if not carefully designed and monitored, could lead to unintended negative consequences.

One of the most significant challenges to the flywheel model is the sheer complexity of the real world. The model assumes a clean, closed-loop system where data flows seamlessly from one stage to the next. But in reality, the learning process is often messy and unpredictable. Learners are not passive recipients of information. They are active agents with their own goals, motivations, and prior knowledge. They learn in a variety of contexts, both formal and informal, and the data that is collected is often incomplete, noisy, and biased. The idea that all of this complexity can be neatly captured and modeled in a single, elegant flywheel is, at best, an oversimplification.

Another potential pitfall of the flywheel model is the risk of over-optimization. The model is designed to optimize for a single goal: improving learner performance. But what if that goal is too narrow? What if, in our relentless pursuit of efficiency and effectiveness, we lose sight of the other important aspects of learning, such as creativity, critical thinking, and collaboration? The flywheel, if not carefully designed, could become a machine for producing highly skilled but narrowly focused automatons, rather than well-rounded, adaptable learners. This is the danger of what has been called "the tyranny of the metric." When we become too focused on a single metric, we can lose sight of the bigger picture.

Finally, the flywheel model raises important questions about equity and access. The model assumes that all learners have access to the same high-quality data, content, and technology. But in reality, there are significant disparities in access to these resources. If the flywheel is only available to a privileged few, it could end up exacerbating existing inequalities, creating a world where the rich get smarter and the poor get left behind. This is the "Matthew effect" in education: "For unto every one that hath shall be given, and he shall have abundance: but from him that hath not shall be taken away even that which he hath." The flywheel, if not carefully implemented, could become a powerful engine for reinforcing existing social and economic inequalities.

In conclusion, while the Adaptive Learning Flywheel is a powerful and compelling model, it is not a panacea. It is a tool, and like any tool, it can be used for good or for ill. The challenge for educators and learning designers is to harness the power of the flywheel while at the same time being mindful of its potential downsides. This means designing systems that are not only efficient and effective, but also humane, equitable, and aligned with the broader goals of education.

---

## Case Study: Duolingo

Duolingo, the language-learning platform with over 500 million users, is a prime example of the Adaptive Learning Flywheel in action. The company has built a powerful data-driven system that is constantly learning and improving, creating a formidable competitive advantage in the crowded language-learning market.

At the heart of Duolingo's success is its massive dataset of learner performance. Every time a user answers a question, completes a lesson, or makes a mistake, that data is captured and fed back into the system. This data is the fuel that powers Duolingo's adaptive engine, a sophisticated machine learning system that the company calls "Birdbrain."

Birdbrain uses this data to personalize the learning experience for each user. It analyzes the user's performance to identify their specific knowledge gaps and misconceptions. It then uses this information to generate a personalized learning path, a sequence of exercises and lessons that is optimized for that user's specific needs. For example, if a user is struggling with a particular verb conjugation, Birdbrain will generate a series of targeted exercises to help them master that concept. This is the power of data-driven personalization in action.

The result of this personalization is better performance. Duolingo users learn faster and more effectively than they would with a traditional, one-size-fits-all approach. This is not just a matter of opinion. Duolingo has conducted numerous studies to prove the effectiveness of its approach. In one study, the company found that 34 hours of Duolingo were equivalent to a full semester of a university language course. This is a remarkable result, and it is a testament to the power of the Adaptive Learning Flywheel.

But the flywheel doesn't stop there. The more effectively users learn, the more data is generated. This data is cleaner, more specific, and more valuable. It feeds back into Birdbrain, which uses it to make even better personalization decisions. The flywheel spins faster. This is the essence of the flywheel effect. It is a self-reinforcing cycle of improvement. The more data Duolingo has, the better it gets at personalizing the learning experience. The better it gets at personalizing, the more effectively users learn. And the more effectively users learn, the more data Duolingo gets. It is a virtuous cycle that is constantly accelerating, constantly improving, and constantly creating more value for the user.

This data flywheel has created a powerful competitive moat for Duolingo. A new entrant to the language-learning market would have a very difficult time replicating Duolingo's success. They would not have the massive dataset of learner performance that Duolingo has accumulated over the years. They would not have the sophisticated machine learning models that Duolingo has developed. And they would not have the brand recognition and user loyalty that Duolingo has built. This is the power of the flywheel. It is a powerful engine for creating a sustainable competitive advantage.

---
